But this one sailor, in the heart of that finality, had raged against the ending with his bare, broken hands. He had introduced a flaw. A splinter of nonsense into the clean sentence of their doom.

Perhaps it was that tiny, meaningless scratch that had saved the IJsvogel from being consumed. That had left it here, a preserved specimen, rather than a dissolved memory. A sentence with a smudge.

“We take him,” Elias said, his voice rough in the absolute quiet. “We take him home.”

They buried the sailor from the IJsvogel at the edge of the open water. They had no shroud but a clean piece of sailcloth. They had no words but the ones they made up, individually, in their heads. Elias spoke the only prayer he could muster.

“You were not finished.”

They committed the body to the deep, black water. It sank slowly, a pale blur swallowed by the dark.

As they turned back to the Willem, the world shifted.

It was not a sound. It was a… tuning. A sense of the air tightening, like a string being drawn to pitch. Elias froze. Every man on the deck froze.

The light changed. The soft grey hardened. The faint blue to the south flickered, like a poorly-trimmed lamp.

On the deck of the Willem, the great blue circle of ice began to glow. Not with its former fierce, narrative light. With a faint, persistent phosphorescence. A bookmark.

From the ridge line of the distant, abandoned temple, a pinprick of light appeared. A single, blue-white star.

It was moving. Not towards them. Not away.

It was walking, with infinite, patient slowness, along the horizon. Pacing the edge of their world. A reader, having found a troublesome, illogical passage, had not closed the book. It had set it aside. And now it was circling back. It had remembered the incomplete sentence. The cacophonous clause. The splinter.

It had all the time in the world. And it lived to finish stories.

The silence returned. But it was a different silence now. It was the silence of a clockwork heard through a wall. The silence of a thing that waits, and calculates, and does not forget.

Elias looked from the pacing star on the horizon to the glowing scar on his deck. He looked at the faces of his men, etched not with collective terror, but with a hundred separate, fierce resolutions. He looked at the derelict IJsvogel, the monument to a clean ending they had refused.

They were not saved. They were simply left hanging.

And a thing left hanging seeks a ground.

“Make sail,” Elias said, his voice carrying in the tuned air. It was not a shout. It was a statement. “South. Towards the blue.”

“The ice in the hull—” Barents began.

“Will hold, or it will melt. The ship will sail, or she will swim. We will live, or we will make a noise they hear in hell.” He turned his back on the pacing star. “We are a splinter in the world’s side. Let us see how far we can travel before it works us out.”

The men moved. Not as one body, but as a crew. A collection of singular wills, aligned. Sails, patched and pathetic, crawled up the yards. Lines were hauled. The Willem groaned, pivoted, and her bowsprit pointed towards the smear of tentative blue.

They left the IJsvogel to its perfect silence. They left the pacing star on the horizon. They took their scar, their damage, their unbearable, beautiful noise, and they sailed into the unfinished water.

The ice did not sing. The water did not speak. The world merely watched, a patient, incomprehensible eye.

And on the deck, amidst the work and the worry, a low sound began. It was not a melody you’d whistle. It was a hum. One man started it, cleaning a rifle. Another, coiling a line, picked it up. Then another. A rough, discordant, utterly human hum. A vibration in the teeth. The sound of a world not holding its breath, but breathing, raggedly, defiantly, on.

Elias stood at the taffrail, the wind needling his left cheek, and listened. To the noise. To the promise within it. The sky ahead was the colour of a new page, blank and full of terror.

They were a sentence no grammar could parse. A story that refused its end.

They were sailing into the margin.

And the thing that lived to complete sentences would, eventually, find a way to finish this one. But they would fill the time until it did with such a glorious, meaningless, human racket that the ending, when it came, would have to shout to be heard.
